
=== persist-001 ===
Q: How do I add persistence to a LangGraph agent?

╭────────────── Answer ──────────────╮
│ To add persistence to a LangGraph  │
│ agent, you can use LangGraph's     │
│ persistence features. This         │
│ involves storing message history   │
│ in a storage backend such as       │
│ SQLite, Postgres, or Azure Cosmos  │
│ DB, and then fetching previous     │
│ messages from state when routing   │
│ to an agent.                       │
│                                    │
│ Here are the steps:                │
│                                    │
│  1 Implement memory in LangGraph   │
│    for AI applications by adding   │
│    persistence to your agents.     │
│  2 Use the AgentState class to     │
│    manage short-term memory,       │
│    specifically the conversation   │
│    history via a messages key.     │
│  3 Extend AgentState to add        │
│    additional fields as needed.    │
│  4 Pass custom state schemas to    │
│    create_agent using the          │
│    state_schema parameter.         │
│  5 When routing to an agent, fetch │
│    previous messages from state    │
│    and selectively include them in │
│    the agent's context.            │
│                                    │
│ By following these steps, you can  │
│ leverage LangGraph's persistence   │
│ features to add long-term memory   │
│ to your agents and improve their   │
│ performance.                       │
╰────────────────────────────────────╯

=== hitl-001 ===
Q: Show me how to implement human-in-the-loop with LangGraph

╭────────────── Answer ──────────────╮
│ To implement human-in-the-loop     │
│ (HIL) features in LangGraph, you   │
│ can follow these steps:            │
│                                    │
│  1 Import the necessary modules    │
│    and initialize the LangGraph    │
│    environment.                    │
│  2 Use the interrupt() function to │
│    pause execution for human       │
│    input. This function takes a    │
│    message as an argument, which   │
│    is displayed to the user.       │
│  3 Handle the user's response by   │
│    using the handle_input()        │
│    function, which returns the     │
│    user's input.                   │
│  4 Resume execution with the       │
│    user's input using the resume() │
│    function.                       │
│                                    │
│ Here's some sample code to get you │
│ started:                           │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│  import langgraph                  │
│                                    │
│  # Initialize LangGraph            │
│  environment                       │
│  langgraph.init()                  │
│                                    │
│  # Define a tool call that         │
│  requires human input              │
│  def my_tool_call():               │
│      # Pause execution for human   │
│  input                             │
│      message = "Please enter your  │
│  response:"                        │
│      langgraph.interrupt(message)  │
│                                    │
│      # Handle user's response      │
│      user_response =               │
│  langgraph.handle_input()          │
│                                    │
│      # Resume execution with       │
│  user's input                      │
│      result =                      │
│  my_tool_call(user_response)       │
│      return result                 │
│                                    │
│  # Call the tool call              │
│  result = my_tool_call()           │
│  print(result)                     │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│ Alternatively, you can create a    │
│ wrapper around your tool to add    │
│ HIL capabilities:                  │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│  import langgraph                  │
│                                    │
│  class MyToolWrapper:              │
│      def __init__(self, tool):     │
│          self.tool = tool          │
│                                    │
│      def call(self):               │
│          # Pause execution for     │
│  human input                       │
│          message = "Please enter   │
│  your response:"                   │
│          langgraph.interrupt(mess  │
│  age)                              │
│                                    │
│          # Handle user's response  │
│          user_response =           │
│  langgraph.handle_input()          │
│                                    │
│          # Resume execution with   │
│  user's input                      │
│          result =                  │
│  self.tool(user_response)          │
│          return result             │
│                                    │
│  # Define the tool call            │
│  def my_tool_call(x):              │
│      # Your tool logic here        │
│      pass                          │
│                                    │
│  # Create a wrapper around the     │
│  tool                              │
│  wrapper =                         │
│  MyToolWrapper(my_tool_call)       │
│                                    │
│  # Call the wrapped tool           │
│  result = wrapper.call()           │
│  print(result)                     │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│ For more information, refer to the │
│ human-in-the-loop guide and the    │
│ Implementing Human-in-the-Loop     │
│ Workflows with Interrupts          │
│ documentation page.                │

Q: How do I handle errors and retries in LangGraph nodes?

╭────────────── Answer ──────────────╮
│ To handle errors and retries in    │
│ LangGraph nodes, you can use one   │
│ of two approaches:                 │
│                                    │
│  1 Write regular Python code       │
│    within your node to catch and   │
│    handle exceptions.              │
│  2 Set a retry_policy to direct    │
│    the graph to retry nodes that   │
│    raise certain types of          │
│    exceptions.                     │
│                                    │
│ Additionally, you can control the  │
│ maximum number of concurrent tasks │
│ by setting max_concurrency in the  │
│ configuration when invoking the    │
│ graph.                             │
╰────────────────────────────────────╯

=== state-001 ===
Q: What are best practices for state management in LangGraph?

╭────────────── Answer ──────────────╮
│ Best practices for state           │
│ management in LangGraph include:   │
│                                    │
│  1 Using a TypedDict, Pydantic     │
│    model, or dataclass to define   │
│    the state.                      │
│  2 Defining and updating state     │
│    using reducers to control how   │
│    state updates are processed.    │
│  3 Utilizing human-in-the-loop     │
│    controls to verify information  │
│    before it is stored in the      │
│    state.                          │
│                                    │
│ These best practices enable        │
│ complex behaviors in chatbots      │
│ without relying solely on message  │
│ lists, allowing for enhanced       │
│ functionality and deeper           │
│ understanding of state management. │
╰────────────────────────────────────╯

=== tools-001 ===
Q: How do I add tools to a LangGraph agent?

╭────────────── Answer ──────────────╮
│ To add tools to a LangGraph agent, │
│ follow these steps:                │
│                                    │
│  1 In the agent editor, scroll to  │
│    the Tools section.              │
│  2 Click + Add tool.               │
│  3 Search for "Slack" and add the  │
│    tools you need:                 │
│     • slack_send_channel_message - │
│       Post messages to a channel   │
│     • slack_reply_to_message -     │
│       Reply in a thread            │
│     • slack_write_private_message  │
│       - Send direct messages       │
│     • slack_read_channel_history - │
│       Read recent messages         │
│     • slack_read_thread_messages - │
│       Read thread replies          │
│                                    │
│ Alternatively, you can also ask    │
│ your agent to add these tools      │
│ itself by typing "Add the Slack    │
│ tools so you can respond to        │
│ messages" in the agent chat.       │
╰────────────────────────────────────╯

│ To stream responses from a         │

=== subgraph-001 ===
Q: How do I create and use subgraphs in LangGraph?

╭────────────── Answer ──────────────╮
│ To create and use subgraphs in     │
│ LangGraph, you need to define how  │
│ the parent graph and the subgraph  │
│ communicate. There are two ways to │
│ do this:                           │
│                                    │
│  1 Shared state schemas: When      │
│    adding a subgraph as a node in  │
│    the parent graph, both the      │
│    parent and subgraph have shared │
│    state keys in their state       │
│    schemas.                        │
│  2 Different state schemas: When   │
│    adding a subgraph as a node in  │
│    the parent graph, there are no  │
│    shared state keys between the   │
│    parent and subgraph schemas.    │
│                                    │
│ To set up LangGraph for            │
│ development, you can install it    │
│ using pip:                         │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│  pip install -U langgraph          │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│ Additionally, you can sign up for  │
│ LangSmith to quickly spot issues   │
│ and improve the performance of     │
│ your LangGraph projects.           │
╰────────────────────────────────────╯

=== memory-001 ===
Q: What's the difference between short-term and long-term memory in LangGraph?

╭────────────── Answer ──────────────╮
│ According to the provided context, │
│ the main differences between       │
│ short-term and long-term memory in │
│ LangGraph are:                     │
│                                    │
│  1 Scope:                          │
│     • Short-term memory is         │
│       thread-scoped, meaning it is │
│       specific to a single thread  │
│       or conversation.             │
│     • Long-term memory is saved    │
│       within custom "namespaces,"  │
│       allowing it to be shared     │
│       across conversational        │
│       threads.                     │
│  2 Purpose:                        │
│     • Short-term memory is used to │
│       remember previous            │
│       interactions within a single │
│       thread or conversation,      │
│       typically for the duration   │
│       of that session.             │
│     • Long-term memory stores      │
│       user-specific or             │
│       application-level data       │
│       across sessions and can be   │
│       recalled at any time in any  │
│       thread.                      │
│  3 Persistence:                    │
│     • Short-term memory is managed │
│       as part of the agent's       │
│       state, persisted via         │
│       thread-scoped checkpoints.   │
│     • Long-term memory uses stores │
│       to save and recall memories, │
│       allowing for more flexible   │
│       management and access        │
│       control.                     │
│  4 Accessibility:                  │
│     • Short-term memory is         │
│       accessible within a single   │
│       thread or conversation.      │
│     • Long-term memory can be      │
│       recalled in any thread,      │
│       making it useful for         │

=== conditional-001 ===
Q: How do I add conditional edges in LangGraph?

╭────────────── Answer ──────────────╮
│ To add conditional edges in        │
│ LangGraph, you can use the         │
│ add_conditional_edges method and   │
│ pass a function that returns a     │
│ list of Send objects or a Command  │
│ object. The function should take   │
│ the current state as an argument   │
│ and return a list of nodes to      │
│ route to.                          │
│                                    │
│ Here is an example:                │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│  def continue_to_jokes(state:      │
│  OverallState):                    │
│      return                        │
│  [Send("generate_joke",            │
│  {"subject": s}) for s in          │
│  state['subjects']]                │
│                                    │
│  graph.add_conditional_edges("nod  │
│  e_a", continue_to_jokes)          │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│ In this example, the               │
│ continue_to_jokes function is      │
│ called with the current state as   │
│ an argument and returns a list of  │
│ Send objects. The                  │
│ add_conditional_edges method then  │
│ uses this list to add conditional  │
│ edges from "node_a" to all nodes   │
│ that match the condition specified │
│ in the list.                       │
│                                    │
│ Alternatively, you can use a       │
│ Command object instead of a list   │
│ of Send objects:                   │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│  def continue_to_jokes(state:      │
│  OverallState):                    │
│      return                        │
│  [Command("generate_joke",         │
│  {"subject": s}) for s in          │
│  state['subjects']]                │
│                                    │
│  graph.add_conditional_edges("nod  │
│  e_a", continue_to_jokes)          │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│ In this example, the               │
│ continue_to_jokes function returns │
│ a list of Command objects instead  │
│ of Send objects. The               │
│ add_conditional_edges method then  │
│ uses this list to add conditional  │
│ edges from "node_a" to all nodes   │
│ that match the condition specified │
│ in the list.                       │
│                                    │
│ Note that you can also use a       │
│ dictionary to specify the          │
│ conditions and corresponding       │
│ nodes:                             │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│  graph.add_conditional_edges("nod  │
│  e_a", {                           │
│      True:                         │
│  Command("generate_joke",          │
│  {"subject": "joke1"}),            │
│      False:                        │
│  Command("generate_joke",          │
│  {"subject": "joke2"})             │
│  })                                │
│                                    │
│                                    │
│ In this example, the               │
│ add_conditional_edges method is    │
│ called with a dictionary that      │
│ specifies the conditions and       │
│ corresponding nodes. The function  │
│ is not needed in this case, as the │
│ list of Command objects is         │
│ specified directly.                │
╰────────────────────────────────────╯

=== parallel-001 ===
Q: Can LangGraph run nodes in parallel? How?

╭────────────── Answer ──────────────╮
│ Yes, LangGraph can run nodes in    │
│ parallel through fan-out and       │
│ fan-in mechanisms, utilizing both  │
│ standard edges and                 │
│ conditional_edges. This allows for │
│ the execution of multiple          │
│ destination nodes simultaneously   │
│ as part of the next superstep,     │
│ improving performance in           │
│ graph-based workflows.             │
╰────────────────────────────────────╯
